--></mo>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<msup>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\frac {c}{n}}\\pm v\\left(1-{\\frac {1}{n^{2}}}\\right)}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle {\\frac {c}{n}}\\pm v\\left(1-{\\frac {1}{n^{2}}}\\right)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/489bb82f15c4e859f93249175b315300df290b9b" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:17.295ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relativistic_aberration_of_light">Relativistic aberration of light</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Special_relativity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Relativistic aberration of light">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Aberration_of_light" title="Aberration of light">Aberration of light</a> and <a href="/wiki/Light-time_correction" title="Light-time correction">Light-time correction</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stellar_aberration_illustration.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="337" data-file-width="301" decoding="async" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Stellar_aberration_illustration.svg/220px-Stellar_aberration_illustration.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Stellar_aberration_illustration.svg/330px-Stellar_aberration_illustration.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Stellar_aberration_illustration.svg/440px-Stellar_aberration_illustration.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stellar_aberration_illustration.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Figure 5-2. Illustration of stellar aberration</div></div></div>\n<p>Because of the finite speed of light, if the relative motions of a source and receiver include a transverse component, then the direction from which light arrives at the receiver will be displaced from the geometric position in space of the source relative to the receiver. The classical calculation of the displacement takes two forms and makes different predictions depending on whether the receiver, the source, or both are in motion with respect to the medium. (1) If the receiver is in motion, the displacement would be the consequence of the <a href="/wiki/Aberration_of_light" title="Aberration of light">aberration of light</a>. The incident angle of the beam relative to the receiver would be calculable from the vector sum of the receiver\'s motions and the velocity of the incident light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mould_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mould-62">[36]</a></sup> (2) If the source is in motion, the displacement would be the consequence of <a href="/wiki/Light-time_correction" title="Light-time correction">light-time correction</a>. The displacement of the apparent position of the source from its geometric position would be the result of the source\'s motion during the time that its light takes to reach the receiver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seidelmann_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seidelmann-63">[37]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The classical explanation failed experimental test. Since the aberration angle depends on the relationship between the velocity of the receiver and the speed of the incident light, passage of the incident light through a refractive medium should change the aberration angle. In 1810, Arago used this expected phenomenon in a failed attempt to measure the speed of light,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferraro_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ferraro-64">[38]</a></sup> and in 1870, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Airy" title="George Airy">George Airy</a> tested the hypothesis using a water-filled telescope, finding that, against expectation, the measured aberration was identical to the aberration measured with an air-filled telescope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dolan_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dolan-65">[39]</a></sup> A "cumbrous" attempt to explain these results used the hypothesis of partial aether-drag,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hollis_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hollis-66">[40]</a></sup> but was incompatible with the results of the <a href="/wiki/Michelson%E2%80%93Morley_experiment" title="Michelson&#8211;Morley experiment">Michelson&#8211;Morley experiment</a>, which apparently demanded <i>complete</i> aether-drag.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[41]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Assuming inertial frames, the relativistic expression for the aberration of light is applicable to both the receiver moving and source moving cases. A variety of trigonometrically equivalent formulas have been published. Expressed in terms of the variables in Fig.&#160;5&#8209;2, these include<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rindler1977_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rindler1977-44">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>57&#8211;60</span></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\cos \\theta \'={\\frac {\\cos \\theta +v/c}{1+(v/c)\\cos \\theta }}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>cos</mi>\n<mo>&#8289;<!-- &#8289; --></mo>\n<mi>&#952;<!-- &#952; --></mi>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\cos \\theta \'={\\frac {\\cos \\theta +v/c}{1+(v/c)\\cos \\theta }}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle \\cos \\theta \'={\\frac {\\cos \\theta +v/c}{1+(v/c)\\cos \\theta }}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/841d5e8247bcff5b34ec7fe4199135c5c803cdc8" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; --></mo>\n<mi>&#952;<!-- &#952; --></mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">]</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\sin \\theta \'={\\frac {\\sin \\theta }{\\gamma [1+(v/c)\\cos \\theta ]}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle \\sin \\theta \'={\\frac {\\sin \\theta }{\\gamma [1+(v/c)\\cos \\theta ]}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/04b1d959a5aa9840f249611011e804cba5079e53" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>&#952;<!-- &#952; --></mi>\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\tan {\\frac {\\theta \'}{2}}=\\left({\\frac {c-v}{c+v}}\\right)^{1/2}\\tan {\\frac {\\theta }{2}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle \\tan {\\frac {\\theta \'}{2}}=\\left({\\frac {c-v}{c+v}}\\right)^{1/2}\\tan {\\frac {\\theta }{2}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/356f6b461b30d528c5a79ad0ab3d0cdc9329043e" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:27.519ex; height:6.676ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relativistic_Doppler_effect">Relativistic Doppler effect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Special_relativity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Relativistic Doppler effect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Relativistic_Doppler_effect" title="Relativistic Doppler effect">Relativistic Doppler effect</a></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Relativistic_longitudinal_Doppler_effect">Relativistic longitudinal Doppler effect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Special_relativity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Relativistic longitudinal Doppler effect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The classical Doppler effect depends on whether the source, receiver, or both are in motion with respect to the medium. The relativistic Doppler effect is independent of any medium. Nevertheless, relativistic Doppler shift for the longitudinal case, with source and receiver moving directly towards or away from each other, is often derived as if it were the classical phenomenon, but modified by the addition of a <a href="/wiki/Time_dilation" title="Time dilation">time dilation</a> term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gill-69">[43]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Assume the receiver and the source are moving <i>away</i> from each other with a relative speed <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle v\\,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle v\\,}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="v\\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8b67d1fd725a759a151374b793113d7a78a65da4" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.515ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> as measured by an observer on the receiver or the source (The sign convention adopted here is that <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle v\\,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle v\\,}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="v\\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8b67d1fd725a759a151374b793113d7a78a65da4" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.515ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> is <i>negative</i> if the receiver and the source are moving <i>towards</i> each other). Assume that the source is stationary in the medium. Then\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle f_{r}=(1-v/c_{s})f_{s}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>f</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>r</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>s</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>f</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>s</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle f_{r}=(1-v/c_{s})f_{s}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle f_{r}=(1-v/c_{s})f_{s}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6cfcca46b9d541aa468cd5eb51ff148140c1582c" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:17.466ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle c_{s}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>s</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle c_{s}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="c_{s}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/75b83e050da28fa0d83e2aa786963805742ab756" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.01ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> is the speed of sound.\n</p><p>For light, and with the receiver moving at relativistic speeds, clocks on the receiver are <a href="/wiki/Time_dilation" title="Time dilation">time dilated</a> relative to clocks at the source. The receiver will measure the received frequency to be\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle f_{r}=\\gamma (1-\\beta )f_{s}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>f</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>r</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>&#947;<!-- &#947; width:14.125ex; height:7.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>where\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\beta =v/c}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#946;<!-- &#946;
